Received

    , Accepted 29th September 2021First published on 30th September 2021AbstractSupercapacitors, also known as electrochemical capacitors, have attracted more and more attention in recent decades due to their advantages of higher power density and long cycle life. For the real application of supercapacitors, there is no doubt that cyclic stability is the most important aspect. As the core component, electrode materials determine the cyclic stability of supercapacitors, although the other components like electrolyte and separator are also important. In this review, we sum up the cyclic stability of supercapacitors according to type of electrode material and its energy storage mechanism, discuss the strategies to boost the stability of those electrode materials, and indicate several key significant considerations in measurement of cyclic stability. The purpose is to obtain safe, long-lasting, and high-performance supercapacitor devices.Qianghong WuQianghong Wu received her B. S. and M. S. degrees from Lanzhou University of Technology. 1. Introduction
Global climate change and energy shortages are forcing people to seek new and renewable energy.1 Inspired by nature, scientists have successfully discovered new renewable energy such as solar, wind, and tidal energy, and applied them in our daily life.2 However, the intermittency of this energy largely limits the scope of practical applications. Therefore, efficient and environmentally friendly energy storage and conversion devices are urgently needed to complete the storage and effective use of these new energy sources. Among many energy and conversion storage devices, electrochemical energy storage devices stand out,3,4 which have become an indispensable renewable energy source in people's daily lives. Electrochemical energy storage devices include various types of batteries and supercapacitors.5 Compared with electrochemical energy storage devices with high specific capacity and excellent rate performance, such as Ni–Zn battery,6 Zn–air battery,7 Zn–ion battery,8 Li–ion battery,9 and aluminum–air battery,10 supercapacitors have insufficient performance in this aspect. Therefore, this is the research object and key technique of future research for supercapacitors. In particular, as a kind of electrochemical energy storage device with excellent performance, supercapacitors have a long cycle life,11,12 higher energy density and power density,13,14 and attracted more and more attention and research interest. Combining the advantages of batteries and supercapacitors, obtaining electrochemical energy storage devices with high specific capacity, high power density and energy density, and long cycle life, has received extensive attention and study. The components of supercapacitors can be generally divided into electrode material, electrolyte, current collector, separator, and so on.15 As an important part of a supercapacitor, electrode materials play a vital role in its performance, which involve carbon material, metal oxide, metal nitride, metal hydroxide, metal carbide, and conductive polymer.15–18 These electrode materials have different energy storage mechanisms, which can be divided into carbon materials with electrical double layered capacitances (EDLCs) behavior, pseudocapacitance produced from conductive polymers and metal nitride/oxide/hydroxide, as well as the battery type behavior by nickel and cobalt based materials.15,19,20
For the practical application, the performance indexes of supercapacitors mainly include specific capacitance, energy density, power density, and cyclic stability. Among them, cyclic stability is a measure of how much capacitance is retained after a certain working time or a certain number of charging-discharging cycles, which is very important to evaluate the service life of supercapacitors.21 Here, we discuss the basic concepts, classification, influencing factors and standard test methods of the cyclic stability of supercapacitors electrode materials in detail. The energy storage mechanism and cyclic stability of various electrode materials and the relationship between them are summarized. The affecting factors and improvement methods for the cyclic stability of electrode materials are presented. Furthermore, the cyclic stability of supercapacitors devices is discussed. It provides the basis for the cyclic stability test of supercapacitors and promotes the development of electrode materials in the supercapacitors industry.
2. Basic information and standard test methods for cyclic stability
As an important index to evaluate the actual service life of supercapacitors, it is necessary to test the cyclic stability. However, there is a lack of uniform specification for testing conditions of cyclic stability for supercapacitors, which makes the evaluation basis of cyclic stability for materials lack of comparability. Therefore, this section mainly introduces the basic information of cyclic stability including its basic concepts, classification, influencing factors and standard test methods.
2.1 Basic concept of cyclic stability
There are few reports on the definition of electrode materials cyclic stability of supercapacitors. Liu et al. consider that cyclic stability of supercapacitors is a measure of how much capacitance is retained after a certain working time or a certain number of charging-discharging cycles.21 There are many factors influencing cyclic stability (see 2.3 for details), among which the setting of test conditions has a great impact on it. For example, when other conditions are same, the same material will show better cyclic stability at a smaller potential window or at a greater current density/scanning rate. Therefore, in order to avoid the occurrence, it is very necessary to standardize its test conditions. Only the cyclic stability obtained under standard and reasonable test conditions can be used to evaluate its actual service life better. Therefore, we think that the cyclic stability of supercapacitors is the capacitance retention rate of the electrode materials or devices under standard and reasonable test conditions, after a certain working time or a certain number of charging-discharging cycles.
2.2 Classification of cyclic stability
Starting from the definition, the cyclic stability of supercapacitor varies with electrode materials. Here, we classify the cyclic stability of supercapacitors on the basis of electrode materials into three categories according to their cyclic trends, which could be summarized as follows, and also shown in Fig. 1.
 Fig. 1  Classification of supercapacitors' cyclic stability: (a) type I with the capacitance retention rate close to 100% after long time cycle. Copyrights of the illustrations cited in type I: carbon material (ref. 22) 2014 Wiley-VCH, metal nitride (ref. 23) 2017 Wiley-VCH, metal carbide (ref. 24) 2016 The Electrochemical Society, metal oxide (ref. 25) 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry, metal hydroxide (ref. 26) 2010 Elsevier. (b) Type II with the capacitance retention rate higher than 100%. Copyrights of the insets cited in type II: metal nitride (ref. 27) 2018 The Royal Society of Chemistry, metal carbide (ref. 28) 2017 American Chemical Society, metal oxide (ref. 29) 2018 American Chemical Society, metal hydroxide (ref. 30) 2012 American Chemical Society. (c) Type III with the continued decline capacitance retention rate. Copyrights of the insets cited in type III: carbon material (ref. 31) 2015 Wiley-VCH, metal nitride (ref. 32) 2018 Elsevier, metal carbide (ref. 33) 2016 Elsevier, metal oxide (ref. 34) 2014 Elsevier, metal hydroxide (ref. 35) 2018 Springer Nature, conductive polymer (ref. 36) 2017 Taylor & Francis Group. 
(i) After charging and discharging processes for a long cyclic number, the specific capacitance of the material has almost no attenuation and the retention rate is close to 100% even after several ten thousand cyclic numbers. The cyclic stability of most carbon materials with EDLCs behavior belongs to this type and is labeled as type I, as shown in Fig. 1a. For example, the biochar of soybean roots porous carbon has good cyclic stability of 98% capacitance retention after 10000 cycles at 5 A g−1 in 6 M KOH.37 Carbon based supercapacitors has the most commercialized prospect in all electrode materials. (ii) Type II as shown in Fig. 1b: in the initial stage of the cyclic stability test or the first half of the charging and discharging cycles, the electrode material will generate a self-activation process, by which the material itself changes from one equilibrium state to another new equilibrium state. In this process, the activity of the excited material increases. Due to the self-activation of the material, the specific capacitance has an obvious increase compared with the initial specific capacitance, and the retention rate is always greater than 100%. After the stage of self-activation, the capacitance retention rate drops to the original capacitance value, or is far less than 100%. For instance, the cyclic stability of CoFe-layered double hydroxides (CoFe-LDHs) belongs to type II, and after 100 cycles, the capacitance retention rate increases firstly; and after that the capacitance retention rate decreases gradually because of some certain reason.30 (iii) Since the beginning of the test, the specific capacitance of electrode materials keeps decreasing continuously. Some decrease extent is not pronounced, even after 10000 cycles, the capacitance retention rate can still reach more than 90% like the cyclic stability of the 2 D MoN film with an excess of 95% of the initial volumetric capacitance after 25000 cycles at a scanning rate of 100 mV s−1 in 1 M H2SO4.38 Some drop very fast. After hundreds of cycles, the capacitance retention rate is less than 30%, such as polypyrrole (PPy) film with only 6.4% capacitance retention rate after 200 cycles at 0.5 A g−1 in 1 M KCl.39 This type of cyclic stability is termed as type III cyclic stability (Fig. 1c). In Fig. 1, we also list several typically electrode materials for each type of cyclic stability of supercapacitances for further understanding.
2.3 Influencing factors of cyclic stability
The cyclic stability of supercapacitors is divided into three types, which are closely related to the influencing factors. The cyclic stability of supercapacitors is influenced by many factors, which is summarized in Fig. 2. First of all, the type of electrode materials directly determines the stability performance because of the different energy storage mechanism, such as carbon materials with EDLCs behavior showing high circulation stability, while metal compounds and conductive polymers with pseudocapacitance or battery behaviors exhibiting weak stability. Secondly, the structure and property of electrode materials also influence the cyclic stability of supercapacitors. Accordingly, a great number of studies have been done to develop advanced electrode materials to boost the stability of supercapacitors. Thirdly, the test condition for stability measurement should be paid great attentions as well as considering the other performance index; those include current density, potential window, scanning rate, test temperature, mass load, electrolyte composition, etc.
 Fig. 2  Influence factors of cyclic stability of supercapacitors. Examples of electrode materials: carbon (Copyright 2013 Wiley-VCH, Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society, Copyright 2012 Wiley-VCH ref. 40–42), conductive polymer (Copyright 2017 Elsevier, ref. 43), metal carbide (Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH, ref. 44), metal nitride (Copyright 2018 The Royal Society of Chemistry, ref. 45), metal hydroxide (Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society, ref. 46) and metal oxide (Copyright 2017 The Royal Society of Chemistry, ref. 47). 

2.3.1 Type of electrode materials. 
Cyclic stability types of the electrode materials are closely related to the type of materials and their energy storage mechanism. (i) Cyclic stability of electrode materials with EDLCs behavior (such as carbon electrode materials) mostly belongs to type I, which is mainly due to the fact that the electrode material does not undergo chemical charge transfer reaction and phase change during the charging and discharging process. That is, charge storage is achieved through the physical process of reversible adsorption and desorption of ions on the electrode/electrolyte interface. Therefore, the capacitance hardly decays during long cycles, and the cyclic stability belongs to type I. (ii) Cyclic stability of battery-type and pseudocapacitance supercapacitor electrode materials (such as metal nitrides, metal oxides/hydroxides and metal carbides and other electrode materials) mostly belong to type II, which is due to the self-activation of the material, the specific capacitance has an obvious increase compared with the initial specific capacitance, and the retention rate is always greater than 100%. After the stage of self-activation, the capacitance retention rate drops to the original capacitance value, or is far less than 100%. (iii) Cyclic stability of conductive polymer electrode materials mostly belong to type III, which is attributed to the mechanism of conductive polymer is rapid and reversible p-type or n-type element doping and counter-doping redox in the polymer. The reaction enables the polymer reach a high charge storage density, generates a Faraday quasi-capacitance, and realizes electric energy storage. The conductive polymer electrode material easily causes the collapse of the material structure due to the violent expansion and contraction of the material volume during the charging and discharging process, so that the capacitance retention rate is less than 100%. Therefore, it is possible to design electrode materials with high specific capacitance, good cyclic stability, and excellent electrochemical performance by optimizing the energy storage mechanism. For the relationship between each electrode material and its cyclic stability (see Section 3).


2.3.2 Influence of test parameters. 
Test conditions (such as potential window and current density/scanning rate) have a great influence on the cyclic stability of electrode materials. For instance, an electrode material shows high specific capacitance at low current density while very weak value at high current density. At low current density/scanning rate, the electrochemical reaction of the electrode material takes place more fully and the electrochemical reaction rate approximately equal the charge transfer rate, thus exhibiting a high specific capacitance. With the progress of the electrochemical reaction, the active material will more or less dissolve in the electrolyte, thereby showing a decrease in cyclic stability. This situation is very normal. However, there are some cases where the current density/scanning rate is deliberately increased to test the cyclic stability. In this case, only a small number of active materials will participate in the electrochemical reaction, and the active materials dissolved during the reaction will be immediately replaced by the materials that did not participate in the reaction, thus exhibiting low specific capacitance and high cyclic stability. Similarly, it may cause difficulties in comparing the data to deliberately select the voltage range where the active material is insoluble in the electrolyte (lower than the normal voltage of the active material) to test the cyclic stability of the electrode material. The parameters of the test cyclic stability should be consistent with that of the CV and GCD tests. During the cyclic stability test, the parameter setting has different influence on each electrode material. Tables 1 and 2 respectively show the influence of current density/scanning rate and potential window on the cyclic stability of different electrode materials.

2.4 Standard test method for cyclic stability
It is regrettable that people always declare pretty high cyclic stability based on the test at very high current density but do not indicate the real capacitance value at this current density. As such, the test condition and the other relative index are the key things for correctly evaluating the supercapacitors stability, and the inconsistent standard for testing the cyclic stability of electrode materials makes the evaluation basis of the cyclic stability of electrode materials lacks comparability. Therefore, this section will introduce a standardized test for the cyclic stability of electrode materials of supercapacitors focusing on the determination of current density, scanning rate, and potential window parameters.
For all electrode materials, the setting of test parameters such as current density/scanning rate and potential window should be as consistent as possible with the parameters used to test other electrochemical properties such as cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charging–discharging (GCD) processes. The potential window should not be reduced or current density/scanning rate should not be increased in order to highlight the performance of cyclic stability. That is to say, within a certain rate performance range, the comparability of materials' cyclic stability obtained by the test is higher. And beyond this range, the specific capacitance of materials will be much lower than the maximum specific capacitance. On the contrary, within this range, the data is comparable even though the ultimate cyclic stability is not ideal. As for the selection of potential window, each electrode materials have a different potential window, and only under the appropriate potential window can correct test data be obtained. The selection of the potential window during the cyclic stability test should be consistent with the CV and GCD tests.
Moreover, in order to verify the correctness of this statement, we deliberately did a set of cyclic stability experiments on Ni(OH)2 electrode material at high current density (10 A g−1) and low current density (1 A g−1). As shown in Fig. 3, when the current density is 10 A g−1, the capacitance retention rate of the electrode material still reaches 100% after 10000 cycles, which belongs to the type I cyclic stability. But its specific capacitance is only 154 F g−1. When the current density is 1 A g−1, the specific capacitance of the electrode material is as high as 1421.8 F g−1 and its capacitance retention rate has dropped to 23.7% after 1200 cycles, which belongs to the type III cyclic stability. For Ni(OH)2 electrode material, as the current density is different during the test, the cycle type will also be different. Therefore, it is very necessary to standardize the test conditions for the cyclic stability of supercapacitor electrode materials, so as to avoid the increase in artificial control to confuse the audience.
 Fig. 3  (a) GCD curves and (b) cyclic stability of Ni(OH)2 electrode materials under different current densities. 
In fact, the phenomenon of improving electrochemical performance through artificial subjective factors also exists in other energy storage systems. Just as Xiao et al. proposed that coulomb efficiency (CE) is usually used to estimate the cycle life of lithium ion batteries, but it is not very effective for monitoring the life of lithium metal batteries (LMBs). Due to the lack of standards related to the actual high-energy LMB, the measured CE itself varies greatly in different groups.71
2.5 Summary
Cyclic stability of supercapacitors is the capacitance retention rate of the electrode materials or devices under standard and reasonable test conditions, after a certain working time or a certain number of charging–discharging cycles. Cyclic stability types of the electrode materials are closely related to the type of materials and their energy storage mechanism. Cyclic stability of electrode materials with EDLCs behavior (such as carbon electrode materials) mostly belongs to type I, which is mainly due to the fact that the electrode material does not undergo chemical charge transfer reaction and phase change during the charging and discharging process. That is, charge storage is achieved through the physical process of reversible adsorption and desorption of ions on the electrode/electrolyte interface. Therefore, the capacitance hardly decays during long cycles, and the cyclic stability belongs to type I. Cyclic stability of battery type and pseudocapacitance supercapacitors electrode materials (such as metal nitrides, metal oxides/hydroxides and metal carbides and other electrode materials) mostly belongs to type II, which is due to the charge storage mechanism of the electrode materials related to the surface-controlled Faraday redox reaction. In the initial stage of the cyclic stability test or the first half of the charging and discharging cycles, due to the self-activation of the material, the specific capacitance has an obvious increase compared with the initial specific capacitance, and the retention rate is always above 100%. After the stage of self-activation, the capacitance retention rate drops to the original capacitance value, or is far less than 100%. Cyclic stability of conductive polymer electrode materials is mostly type III, which is attributed to the mechanism of conductive polymer is rapid and reversible p-type or n-type element doping and counter-doping redox in the polymer. The reaction enables the polymer to reach a high charge storage density, generates a Faraday quasi-capacitance, and realizes electric energy storage. The conductive polymer electrode material easily causes the collapse of the material structure due to the violent expansion and contraction of the material volume during the charging and discharging process, so that the capacitance retention rate is less than 100%. Therefore, it is possible to design electrode materials with high specific capacitance, good cyclic stability and excellent electrochemical performance by optimizing the energy storage mechanism. The main influencing factors of cyclic stability are the type of electrode materials and test parameters, which include current density, potential window, scanning rate, test temperature, mass load, electrolyte composition, etc. When other test conditions are appropriate, the current density/scanning rate for testing cyclic stability should be as close as possible to the current density/scanning rate when the maximum specific capacitance is obtained. The selection of the potential window during the cyclic stability test should be consistent with the CV and GCD tests.
3. Cyclic stability of different electrode materials and thier energy storage mechanism
There are many types of electrode materials for supercapacitors, including metal nitrides, metal carbides, metal oxides, metal hydroxides, metal sulfides, metal selenides, metal phosphides, conductive polymers, and carbon materials. This section will introduce the energy storage mechanism and cyclic stability of each electrode material in detail. According to different energy storage mechanisms, supercapacitor electrode materials can be divided into EDLCs, pseudocapacitors and battery-type behaviors.3,20,21,72 The schematic diagrams of the CV curves and GCD curves are shown in Fig. 4. The energy storage process of EDLCs is formed by the reversible adsorption/desorption process of electrolyte ions on the electrode/electrolyte interface, and does not involve electrochemical reactions and phase transitions, so the cyclic stability is generally good. The CV and GCD curves of this type of electrode materials are shown in Fig. 4a. Pseudocapacitors include surface redox pseudocapacitors and intercalation pseudocapacitors. The former stores charge through the oxidation–reduction reaction of anions and cations in the electrolyte on the surface of the electrode materials; while, the latter stores energy through the insertion/extraction of cations in the electrolyte into the crystal structure of the electrode material, which does not undergo phase change during this process. Compared with EDLCs electrode materials, the specific capacitance of these materials is generally larger, and the cyclic stability is relatively poor but not bad. Their CV and GCD curves (Fig. 4b and c) are similar to those of the EDLCs. The electrochemical reaction of battery-type electrode materials is controlled by electrolyte ion diffusion, which usually undergoes intercalation and/or alloying reactions of phase change ions. Therefore, the CV curve will have obvious faradaic redox peaks, and the GCD curve will have charging/discharging plateaus72,73 (Fig. 4d). Battery-type electrode materials have high energy density, but due to the slow material phase change kinetics involved in the charging and discharging process, they exhibit poor cyclic stability.
 Fig. 4  Comparison of the electrochemical behavior for (a) EDLCs, (b) and (c) pseudocapacitor, and (d) battery-type materials. (up: CV curves; and down: GCD curves). Figure copyrights: copyright 2016 Springer (ref. 3), copyright 2019 Zhengzhou University (ref. 20), Copyright 2020 Wiley Online Library (ref. 21), copyright 2018 American Chemical Society (ref. 72). 
3.1 Metal nitrides
The high theoretical capacitance of the metal nitrides (VN has a capacitance of 1340 F g−1 at 2 mV s−1 (ref. 78)) as the anode material for supercapacitors has been widely studied in recent decades, involving niobium nitride (NbN),79 chromium nitride (CrN),80–82 molybdenum nitride (MoN),38,48 titanium nitride (TiN),23,32,49,50,74,83–87 iron nitride (Fe2N),88 cobalt nitride (Co3N),27 ferrous nitride (Fe3N),89 vanadium nitride (VN),51–53,78,90–92 and so on. Fig. 5 shows several metal nitrides electrode materials. Nevertheless, it is difficult to reach the practical application standard after the long-term test due to their specific charge storage mechanism different from that of carbon materials based on electric double layers.
 Fig. 5  (a) SEM and HR-TEM images of the TiN nanosheet arrays. Reproduced with permission from ref. 74. Copyright 2018 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (b) Controlled conversion of FeOOH nanorods to Fe2N as asymmetric supercapacitor electrode. Reproduced with permission from ref. 75. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. (c) Schematic illustration of the favorable features of VNNDs/CNSs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 76. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (d) SEM images of Ti@TiO2/MoO3 and Ti@TiN/MON electrodes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 77. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. 
Based on the results in literature, it can be known that most of the cyclic stability of metal nitrides belongs to the type III. Moreover, the different cyclic stability of the same nitrides had been reported inconsistently in the existing literature, which leads researchers to think what are the factors that affect the cyclic stability of nitrides? Choi et al. believed that the factors affected the cyclic stability of metal nitrides include crystal size, material load, electrolyte concentration, and potential window.78 VN is used as an example. Liu et al. used commercial VN as electrode materials, which only can maintain about 15% of the initial capacitance after 2000 cycles in 6 M KOH electrolyte at a scanning rate of 30 mV s−1.93 While in the same electrolyte and under the similar potential window, Balamurugan and his colleagues were able to make the cycle number of VN last up to 10000 (current density is 10 A g−1).76 Zhou et al. obtained that the capacitance retention rate of VN reached 88% after 500 cycles and the rate performance was 65% at 50 mV s−1, that the maximum specific capacitance was 247.5 F g−1 at 2 mV s−1 (ref. 49) (Fig. 6a and b). However, when testing the cyclic stability, the scanning rate is 100 mV s−1. If the scanning rate is 50 mV s−1 or the material rate capability of 100 mV s−1 is used for the cyclic test, the results will be more satisfactory. Choi et al. tested the cyclic stability of VN, the rate capability at 100 mV s−1 is 41.3% (the maximum specific capacitance is 1340 F g−1 at 2 mV s−1), and the material cyclic stability at 50 mV s−1 after 1000 cycles is 90%78 (Fig. 6c and d). The effect of current density on the cyclic stability of nitrides is reflected in various commonly used nitrides like TiN, and MoN, not just in VN. As we all know, VN uses the fast and reversible redox reaction on the surface to complete the storage of charge. At high current density, there are few active substances that can participate in the redox reaction, which leads to the rapid decline of capacitance. And when only a fraction of the active material is involved in the reaction, the portion of the material that is dissolved after the reaction is immediately replenished by the active material that is not involved in the reaction. Thus, it is only reliable to test the VN's cyclic stability at a current density where the specific capacitance is not significantly attenuated compared to the highest capacitance value.
 Fig. 6  (a) Specific capacitances of the electrodes with TiN, VN, and TiN–VN fibers respectively at progressive scanning rates from 2 mV s−1 to 50 mV s−1. (b) Cycling performance of composite core–shell structured TiN–VN fibers under a scanning rate of 100 mV s−1. (a and b) Reproduced with permission from ref. 49. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society. (c) Specific capacitance versus scanning rate with different active-material loading. (d) Cyclic behavior of VN nanocrystals synthesized at 400 °C scanned at 50 mV s−1 up to 1000 cycles. (c and d) Reproduced with permission from ref. 78. Copyright 2006 Wiley-VCH. 
Besides the current density and scanning rate, the working voltage range of the nitrides in the test process also affect its cyclic stability. This is because the redox reactions of the nitrides under different potential windows are different. Therefore, the results obtained by using the potential range where the reaction product is not dissolved in the electrolyte to test the cycle performance are significantly better than the results obtained by using the potential window consistent with the CV and GCD curves to test the cycle performance. Choi studied VN's cyclic stability under different potential window. And found that when the potential window was determined to be −1.2 to −0.3 V, after 1000 cycles the cyclic stability of VN was much higher than the result when the voltage window was −1.2 to 0 V. In their work, they chose −1.2 to 0 V for the test potential windows of CV and GCD curves.78 This also indicates that the appropriate potential window is crucial for redox reactions. Thus, it is also necessary to ensure that the potential window in the cyclic stability test is consistent with that in the CV and GCD tests. Shu94 and Xu92 also reported the exploration of different potential windows in their own work, and they got the same results as Choi. In addition to the study of VN, Ouendi and his colleagues, and Wang et al. also investigated the cyclic stability of WN and TiN under different potential windows,95,96 and cyclic stability of the same nitride under different potential windows is shown in Table 3.

3.2 Metal carbides
Metal carbides are formed by integrating carbon atoms into the gaps of their parent metals. They are generally considered to be a special type of interstitial compound with high melting point, high conductivity, and excellent mechanical and chemical stability, and good corrosion resistance.55,101 Metal carbides includes titanium carbide (Ti3C2),24,102–105 thallium carbide (TaC),33 molybdenum carbide (Mo2CTX/Mo2C),28,99,106–108 niobium carbide (NbC)100 and MXene (Ti3C2Tx),54,55,98,108–113etc.Fig. 7 shows some typical metal carbides applied as electrode materials in supercapacitors. For carbides, the surface termination of –F and –OH impedes the transport of electrolyte ions and is a key factor affecting the capacitance retention. Therefore, general formula is generally written as Mn+1XnTX (n = 1–3), where M represents metal atoms (Ti, Mo or V, etc.), X represents carbon atoms, and TX represents surface functional groups (such as hydroxyl, oxygen, fluorine, and so on). For the sake of simplicity, TX is often omitted, but this does not mean that there are no surface functional groups.114,115
 Fig. 7  (a) SEM and TEM images of Ti3C2TX nanoflakes at different magnifications. Reproduced with permission from ref. 98. Copyright 2017 Elsevier. (b) SEM images of Mo2C/NCF. Reproduced with permission from ref. 99. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (c) SEM and TEM micrographs (inset) of NbC-CDC fiber. Reproduced with permission from ref. 100. Copyright 2016 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
As a new type of two-dimensional material, the energy storage mechanism of carbides is similar to that of metal nitrides. The difference is that the two-dimensional layer structure can ensure the adsorption and rapid transport of electrolyte ions, providing more electric double layer capacitance for the material, which makes carbide excellent electrochemical performance. Most metal carbides exhibit good cyclic stability, which belongs to Type II and Type III. However, the influence of current density/scanning rate on cyclic stability cannot be ignored. Ti3C2TX, as the most common type of carbide, has been extensively studied in recent decades. Gogotsi and colleagues116 obtained the mechanism of Ti3C2TX providing capacitance by in situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) in conjunction with electrochemical experiments. The pseudocapacitance of Ti3C2TX was derived from the redox reaction of Ti atoms on the surface. The reversible redox reaction on the surface made the effect of current density/scanning rate on carbides consistent with that of metal nitrides. Jiang et al.54 monitored the cyclic stability of Ti3C2TX in the H2SO4 electrolyte at the current density of 20 A g−1, and found that after 20000 cyclic tests, the material was still able to maintain 98% of the initial capacity. In contrast, the Ti3C2TX reported in Peng's55 work had a capacity of only 5000 cycles at a current density of 4 A g−1, and had been reduced to 70% of its initial value. We think the reason for this is the same as nitrides. And we can see from Table 4 that this phenomenon is reflected in all kinds of carbides. Similarly, the selection of potential window in the testing process will also affect the cyclic stability of carbides. For instance, in the report of Li and colleagues109 on Ti3C2TX, it was found that when the current density was 1 A g−1 and the potential window was −1.2 to −0.3 V, after 10000 cyclic tests, only 34.9% of the initial capacitance could be maintained. However, when Xu et al.98 adjusted the operating potential range to −0.75 to 0.25 V, the specific capacitance of the Ti3C2TX did not decline after the same long-term test. As shown in Table 4, it's not hard to see that this is common for other carbides (Fig. 7).

3.3 Metal oxides
Metal oxides include nickel oxide (NiO),34,56–58,124–128 cobalt oxide (Co3O4),119,129,130 manganese oxide (Mn3O4, and MnO2),47,131–139 iron oxide (Fe3O4, Fe2O3, and α-Fe2O3),120,140–143 vanadium oxide (V2O5),29,59,60,121,144,145 molybdenum oxide (MoO2, MoO3−X, HXMoO3, and MoO3),25,146–150 titanium dioxide (TiO2)123,151–153 and copper oxide (CuO)61–63,154–159etc. (Fig. 8); it is a controversial material, because there are both pseudocapacitive behavior and battery-type behavior during the charging and discharging process. A detailed discussion of pseudocapacitive electrode materials and battery-type electrode materials can be found in these classic documents.3,21 The cyclic stability of metal oxides differs depending on the type of material, which exists in the three types (Type I, II, and III) of cyclic stability. Taking MnO2 as an example, it is well known that the capacitance of manganese oxide mainly comes from the pseudocapacitance, which is a reversible redox transition caused by proton and/or cation exchange in the electrolyte, and Mn (III)/Mn (II), Mn (IV)/Mn (III), Mn (VI)/Mn (IV) caused by the transition. The specific mechanism is as eqn (1):15,160 MnOα(OC)β + δC+ + δe+ ↔ MnOα−β(OC)β+δ(1)where C+ denotes the protons and alkali metal cations (Li+, Na+, K+) in the electrolyte, and MnOα(OC)β and MnOα−β(OC)β+δ indicate MnO2·nH2O in high and low oxidation states, respectively. From eqn (1), we can see that there are both cations and electrons in the redox process. Therefore, when the current density/scanning rate is large, the redox reaction rate is much lower than the electron transport rate for MnO2 with poor conductivity. This allows fewer materials to supply the capacitance, resulting in a rapid drop to a smaller value than the capacitance. If the current density/scanning rate is used to test the cyclic stability, the phenomenon that the capacitance value is very small and the cyclic stability is very superior will exist, as mentioned in the work of Bag, and Huang.131,132 On the contrary, in the reports of Xia and colleagues,133 if the cycle performance is studied with a smaller current density/scanning rate, the stability of MnO2 is far worse than that obtained by Bag's and Huang's results.
In addition to MnO2, other oxides also have similar situations. For example, the capacitance retention of the Ni–Co–O@CFP electrode reaches 93.65% after 5000 cycles at a high current density of 20 A g−1 (the rate capability is 85% at 10 A g−1)161 (Fig. 9a–c). The electrochemical performance of the Ni–Co–O@CFP is very good, but it would be more perfect if it shows rate capability of 20 A g−1 and then test the cyclic stability of the material, or test the cyclic stability of the material at 10 A g−1. Cao and co-workers test the electrochemical performance of porous NiO nanotube arrays, that rate capability is 84% at 40 A g−1, and the capacitance retention rate is still 93.2% at 2 A g−1 after 10000 cycles34 (Fig. 9d and e). Poor conductivity is a common problem of metal oxides, so this phenomenon can be observed not only in MnO2, but also in the commonly used ones like V2O5, Fe2O3, and Fe3O4. Therefore, we believe that the premise of characterizing the cyclic stability of pseudocapacitive metal oxides is to ensure that the current density/scanning rate used can basically cause the redox reaction of all electrode material. As is known to all, metal oxide as a kind of pseudocapacitive material is one of the prerequisites of the metal elements that must have more than one valence (i.e., the redox reaction can happen) then for more valence metal elements (such as V, Mn, Fe, and so on), will appear different potential window, change of valence state in the process of reaction (i.e., product) is not the same as this will lead to cycle on the stability difference for this. We also made a summary of the situation of oxides, as shown in Table 5.

3.4 Metal hydroxides
Metal hydroxide is an ionic compound composed of positively charged metal ions and negatively charged hydroxide ions. The electrostatic interaction makes the metal positive ion and hydroxide negative ion form a firm ionic bond. The s orbital of metal hydroxides is usually saturated, so most metal hydroxides have good thermal and chemical stability. However, their d orbitals may be unsaturated, thus endowing them with some unique properties that make them have great application potential in the field of electronic devices.166,167 These unique properties include a wide energy bandgap,70,168 a high dielectric constant,164,169,170 a reactive electron transition capability,66,171 good electricity,65,172 and superconductivity.173,174 It is based on these excellent characteristics that the metal hydroxide has good cyclic stability, most of which belongs to Type II and Type III. For example, the cyclic stability of a small part of Ni–Mn LDHs and Ni–Al LDHs belongs to Type II, and the cyclic stability of some other materials such as nickel hydroxide (Ni(OH)2),168,175 nickel cobalt hydroxide (Ni–Co(OH)2)176–178 and magnesium nickel hydroxide (Mg–Ni(OH)2)179etc. belongs to Type III. The classification of metal hydroxides is shown in Fig. 10.
 Fig. 10  (a) TEM and SEM images of NiAl-LDH hollow microsphere. Reproduced with permission from ref. 30. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. (b) Schematic representation of the synthesis route from Zn-NFA to LDH-NFA. Reproduced with permission from ref. 65. Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c) Cycling performance and coulombic efficiencies of A-Ni5Co5-LDH/NF at a current density of 20 A g−1 for 10000 cycles. Reproduced with permission from ref. 163. Copyright 2017 Elsevier. (d) SEM images of NiMn-LDH-H. Reproduced with permission from ref. 164. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. (e) SEM and HRTEM images of Layered Co–Mn hydroxide grown on flexible CC. Reproduced with permission from ref. 165. Copyright 2016 Springer. 
The typical example of metal hydroxide used in supercapacitors is LDHs, as shown in Table 6. LDHs are an anionic intercalating material with a large specific surface area that can be synthesized according to specific functional requirements. The basic structural formula is:[M1−x2+Mx3+(OH)2]x+Ax/nn−·mH2O

Among them, M2+ is the divalent metal (Fe2+, Mn2+, Zn2+, Mg2+, Co2+, Ni2+etc.) on the main plate layer, M3+ is the trivalent metal (Al3+, Co3+, Mn3+, Fe3+, Cr3+etc.) on the plate layer, An− is an interlayer anion (Cl−, NO3−, OH−, SO42−etc.), x is the mass ratio of M2+/(M2++M3+), and its value is generally about 0.16–0.33, and m represents the number of moles of crystalline water per mole of LDHs.180–182 In the structure of LDHs, trivalent cations (M3+) replace part of divalent cations (M2+) to increase the positive charge. Therefore, it is necessary to introduce anions into the structural unit to maintain a balance with the positive charge to maintain the stability of the crystal structure. This makes LDHs have a variety of unique physical and chemical properties, including electricity, the appearance memory effect of structural layers, and the exchange of ions between layers.183–187 Due to the high oxidation state of metal cations, this kind of layered material has good conductivity, which is conducive to the rapid transmission of electrons and electrolyte ions. With the appropriate synthesis method, LDHs can achieve a relatively high specific capacity and long-term cyclic stability of more than 10000 cycles.188,189 For example, Ni–Al LDHs, which capacitance retention can reach 91.9% (initial specific capacitance ∼2200 F g−1) after 100000 cycles in 2 M KOH electrolyte, at 5 A g−1 and (0–0.5) V.174
Other metal hydroxides, take Ni(OH)2 for example, in the case of similar test conditions such as electrolyte and potential window, Ni(OH)2 shows good cyclic stability at high current density (32 A g−1), and it could still reach 96% after 10000 cycles. But at low current density (5 A g−1), it is only 53% after 2000 cycles.168,175 Although metal hydroxides show good cyclic stability, the parameter setting in the cyclic stability test still has an important influence on the cyclic stability of metal hydroxides. The specific capacitance of two materials of Co–Fe LDHs at 1 A g−1 is 728 F g−1 (ref. 190) and 145 F g−1,191 respectively, and the different capacitances of them may be greatly related to the selection of potential window. The former has a capacitance retention rate of 84% after 5000 cycles at 2 A g−1, while the latter has a capacitance retention rate of 110% after 100 cycles at 10 A g−1 as shown in Fig. 11. It is worth mentioning that some metal oxides and metal hydroxides are also used as electrode materials for battery-type supercapacitors, among which nanometer nickel and cobalt base materials are typical electrode materials for battery-type supercapacitors. The materials that exhibit phase change behavior during electrochemical charging and discharging belong to battery type materials. Compared with pseudocapacitive materials, electrochemical reactions of battery-type materials are controlled by electrolyte ion diffusion, and phase change ion embedding and/or alloying reactions usually occur. Battery-type materials usually have high charging and discharging capacity, but due to the slow phase change kinetics during charging and discharging, their rate performance is low, and the cyclic stability is not ideal at low current density/scanning rate.11,192
 Fig. 11  (a) Specific capacitance of the material at different discharge current densities, and (b) specific capacitance vs. cycle number at a current density of 10 A g. (a and b) Reproduced with permission from ref. 190. Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c). Specific capacitance of the Co–Fe LDHs with different Co/Fe ratios at various current densities, and (d) cycling performances of Co–Fe LDHs with different Co/Fe ratios at a current density of 2 A g. (c and d) Reproduced with permission from ref. 191. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. 
3.5 Conductive polymers
As another important pseudocapacitive electrode material, conductive polymer is a kind of synthetic polymer with long chain conjugated structure. Its conductivity is between semiconductor and metal, even comparable to metal. Therefore, conductive polymers have been widely used in supercapacitor electrode materials in recent years. However, conductive polymer electrode materials are prone to structural collapse during the cycle test, so its cyclic stability generally belongs to Type III. According to the conductive mechanism, conductive polymers can be divided into three categories: electron conductive polymers (e.g. polyaniline, polypyrrole, polythiophene etc.), ionic conductive polymers (poly(4-vinyl pyridine)) and redox conductive polymers (e.g. polyethylene ferrocene, poly(o-phenylenediamine))35,201–206 (Fig. 12). The mechanism of conductive polymer is that rapid and reversible p-type or n-type element doping and de-doping redox reactions occur in the polymer; the p-type doping of the conductive polymer is the loss of electrons in its main chain accompanied by the embedding of anions, while the n-type doping is the gain of electrons in its main chain accompanied by the embedding of cations.68,207 So that conductive polymer achieves a high charge storage density, generates a high Faraday quasi capacitor and achieves electric energy storage.
 Fig. 12  (a) SEM of the APPH-2 stretched at the strain of 100%. Reproduced with permission from ref. 200. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. (b) SEM and TEM micrographs of Pani-ND@GN composite. Reproduced with permission from ref. 201. Copyright 2019 Springer. (c) SEM images of hydrothermally polymerized PEDOT nanostructures attired CFC. Reproduced with permission from ref. 43. Copyright 2017 Elsevier. (d) SEM image of internal porous structures of the dehydrated hydrogel and the enlarged observation reflects that the hydrogel scaffold is composed of nanoparticles resulting from the crystallization of PANI. Reproduced with permission from ref. 202. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. 
The current density/scanning rate plays an important role in the electrochemical performance measurement of conductive polymer electrode materials. As the scanning rate increases, the specific capacitance value decreases and the capacitance retention rate increases. At the high scanning rate, due to the diffusion effect of ions in the electrode, some active sites cannot complete the redox transition. The decrease of capacitance indicates that part of electrode surface cannot be contacted under high charging and discharging rate, which shows the illusion of increasing capacitance retention rate. Therefore, the specific capacitance obtained at a slower scanning rate is considered to be close to these obtained when the electrode material is fully utilized, and the capacitance retention rate obtained at this time has higher comparability.
Dual et al. studied that the capacitance retention rate of PPy was 85% at 100 mV s−1 after 5000 cycles, and the rate capability reached 72.9% at 50 mV s−1 (ref. 208) (Fig. 13a and b). Yuan et al. found that the capacitance retention rate of PPy was 96.3% at 10 mV s−1 after 1000 cycles, and the rate capability was 51% at 100 mV s−1 (ref. 209) (Fig. 13c and d). From the aspect of test parameter setting, if the former can give such cyclic performance within 50 mV s−1 or also show the rate capability of the material at 100 mV s−1, then the result will be better. Fu et al.210 tested the cycling performance of PPy, in 1 M Na2SO4 electrolyte, the specific capacitance was 449.8 F g−1 at 1 A g−1, the rate performance was 66.7% at 20 A g−1, and the capacitance retention rate was 50.9% at 10 A g−1 after 10000 cycles.
 Fig. 13  (a) Variation of specific capacitances of PPy nanobelts, nanobricks, and nanosheets with scanning rate. (b) Variation of specific capacitance with respect to cycle numbers. Inset shows the CV curves of PPy nanosheets at different cycles in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte. (a and b) Reproduced with permission from ref. 208. Copyright 2012 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (c). Variation of the specific capacitance with respect to the scanning rate. (d) cycle life of the PPy and Ag/PPy electrodes. (c and d) Reproduced with permission from ref. 209. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. 
The applied potential has a significant effect on the conductivity of the conductive polymer. The increase of applied potential is beneficial to the deep doping of the polymer, thus improving the conductivity of the conducting polymer. However, when the applied potential is too high, it will lead to the degradation and over oxidation of the conductive polymer chain, resulting in the decrease of its conductivity. Therefore, each conductive polymer has a potential threshold for its highest conductivity. Charging a conducting polymer beyond the upper limit of its potential window may permanently impair the electrochemical activity of the conducting polymer. This process is called peroxidation. The mechanism of peroxidation and its effect on the structure–conductivity relationship are complex and related to polymers. However, the ultimate result of the peroxide reaction is to reduce the conductivity of the conducting polymer, making it an electrochemical inert substance.211,212 The cyclic stability of different conductive polymers as shown in Table 7.

3.6 Carbon materials
Carbon materials have been widely used in supercapacitors due to their characteristics of light weight, abundant pore structure, good chemical stability and low manufacturing cost. Common carbon materials used in EDLCs include: active carbon, graphene, and carbon nanotubes etc.224–230 (Fig. 14). Carbon materials used in supercapacitor mainly follow the working principle of EDLCs: the surface charge of the electrode and the ions in the electrolyte are absorbed to its surface through electrostatic interaction, and an interface layer is formed between the electrode and the electrolyte which is equivalent and opposite electrical properties to the surface charge of the electrode. When an electric field is applied to both ends of the supercapacitor, the anions and cations in the electrolyte move to the positive and negative poles respectively, forming the potential difference. The result is a double layer. When the electric field is withdrawn, the double layer is stable because the charge on the electrode attracts the opposite charge in the electrolyte. After connecting the two poles of the supercapacitor, the charge on the electrode moves at the left and right side of the potential, generating current in the outer circuit, and the electrolyte becomes electrically neutral again. Both of them belong to the working principle of the EDLCs,231–233 and type I cyclic model.
 Fig. 14  (a) Molecular models showing different types of carbon nanomaterials categorized by their dimensionality. It includes fullerenes, nanotubes, nanoribbons, graphene, nanotube networks, etc. Reproduced with permission from ref. 224. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. (b) SEM images of the polymer and carbon membranes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 225. Copyright 2019 Springer. (c) Schematic illustration for the synthesis process of PPC-N&Ps. (d). SEM images of PP precursor and PPC900-N&P30, and elemental mapping images for C, N, and P elements. (c and d) Reproduced with permission from ref. 226. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (e). SEM and TEM images of EGO, CG-4 and KG-7. Reproduced with permission from ref. 227. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. (f) SEM, TEM and HRTEM images of N-CNT, MnO2@N-CNT and MnO2 on MnO2@N-CNT. Reproduced with permission from ref. 228. Copyright 2015 Elsevier. 
According to the statistical data in the Table 8 (of course, the large number of reference materials we provide is not meant to be comprehensive). No matter the current density/scanning rate is large or small, the cyclic stability of carbon materials is very good, which related to the energy storage mechanism of carbon materials for double layer capacitance. Compared with pseudocapacitor electrode materials that store charges through electrochemically active material adsorption/desorption or electrochemical redox reaction, the current density/scanning rate has a smaller effect on the cycle stability of carbon materials. However, the standardized testing of cycle stability in carbon materials still cannot be ignored.

3.7 Other electrode materials
In addition to the several electrode materials described above, there are more and more researches on the use of metal sulfides, phosphides and selenides as electrode materials for supercapacitors. Metal sulfides mainly include cobalt sulfides (CoSx),247 nickel sulfides (Ni3S2),248 ternary nickel cobalt sulfide (NiCo2S4)249etc. Metal selenides include cobalt–selenium compounds (CoSe/CoSe2),250,251 copper–cobalt–selenium compounds (CuCoSe4),252 nickel–cobalt–selenium compounds (H–Ni–Co–Se)253 and nickel–selenium compounds (NiSe2/Ni3Se2)254,255etc. Metal phosphides mainly include nickel–phosphorus compounds (Ni2P),256 nickel–cobalt–phosphorus compounds (Ni–Co–P),257 sulfur-doped cobalt–phosphorus compounds (Co(P, S))258 and cobalt–phosphorus compounds (CoP)258etc. Among these materials, most of the cyclic stability curves belong to Type II and Type III. Similarly, the cyclic stability of metal sulfide, metal selenide, and metal phosphide electrode materials with pseudocapacitive behavior is also affected by the parameter settings during the test, as shown in Table 9. Herein, metal sulfides, metal phosphides and metal selenides will not be discussed in detail. Some comprehensive reviews about other electrode materials can be found in the literature.21,188
